<p><b>Brian Anthony Boitano</b> (born October 22, 1963)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> is an American <a href="/wiki/Figure_skating" title="Figure skating">figure skater</a> from <a href="/wiki/Sunnyvale,_California" title="Sunnyvale, California">Sunnyvale, California</a>. He is the <a href="/wiki/Figure_skating_at_the_1988_Winter_Olympics" title="Figure skating at the 1988 Winter Olympics">1988 Olympic champion</a>, the 1986 and 1988 <a href="/wiki/World_Figure_Skating_Championships" title="World Figure Skating Championships">World Champion</a>, and the 1985–1988 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Figure_Skating_Championships" title="United States Figure Skating Championships">U.S. National Champion</a>. He turned professional following the 1988 season. He returned to competition in 1993 and competed at the <a href="/wiki/1994_Winter_Olympics" title="1994 Winter Olympics">1994 Winter Olympics</a>, where he placed sixth.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Boitano&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Brian Boitano was born in <a href="/wiki/Mountain_View,_California" title="Mountain View, California">Mountain View, California</a>, and as an adult has lived in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Boitano is a graduate of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marian_A._Peterson_High_School" title="Marian A. Peterson High School">Marian A. Peterson High School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sunnyvale,_California" title="Sunnyvale, California">Sunnyvale, California</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson-3">[3]</a></sup>He is of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_American" title="Italian American">Italian American</a> descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Figure_skating_career">Figure skating career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Boitano&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Figure skating career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brian_Boitano_1990_Paraguay_stamp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="297" data-file-width="237" decoding="async" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Brian_Boitano_1990_Paraguay_stamp.jpg/220px-Brian_Boitano_1990_Paraguay_stamp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1d/Brian_Boitano_1990_Paraguay_stamp.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brian_Boitano_1990_Paraguay_stamp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Boitano on a 1990 Paraguay stamp</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_career">Early career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Boitano&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Brian Boitano first made his mark on the international scene when he won the bronze medal at the 1978 <a href="/wiki/World_Junior_Figure_Skating_Championships" title="World Junior Figure Skating Championships">World Junior Figure Skating Championships</a>, beating future rival <a href="/wiki/Brian_Orser" title="Brian Orser">Brian Orser</a> for that medal<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Boitano was known primarily as a jumper early in his career and he, along with several other skaters, helped push the technical envelope of men's skating.  In 1982 Boitano became the first American to land a <a href="/wiki/Axel_jump" title="Axel jump">triple Axel</a>. In 1987 he introduced his signature jump, the 'Boitano triple <a href="/wiki/Lutz_jump" title="Lutz jump">Lutz</a>' in which the skater raises his left arm above his head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7">[7]</a></sup> He attempted a quadruple jump throughout the 1986–87 season and at the 1988 <a href="/wiki/World_Figure_Skating_Championships" title="World Figure Skating Championships">World Figure Skating Championships</a>, but did not cleanly land the jump; he double-footed the landing on two occasions.
</p><p>At the 1983 World Championships, he became the first skater to ever land all six triple jumps in competition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7">[7]</a></sup>  Indeed, he would eventually include and successfully land eight triple jumps in his free skate program<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>, the maximum number possible (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zayak_rule" title="Zayak rule">Zayak rule</a>). He would jump two flip jumps and two triple Axels to compete with his rival, Brian Orser who jumped one triple flip and one triple Axel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7">[7]</a></sup> It was not until his failure to defend his World title in 1987 that he focused specifically on improving his artistry.  Towards this end, he worked with renowned choreographer Sandra Bezic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Boitano placed second at the 1984 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Figure_Skating_Championships" title="United States Figure Skating Championships">United States Figure Skating Championships</a>, earning himself a trip to the <a href="/wiki/1984_Winter_Olympics" title="1984 Winter Olympics">1984 Winter Olympics</a>. He placed 5th at the Olympics, setting the stage for his success over the next four years.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_Champion">World Champion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Boitano&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: World Champion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the 1984 Olympics, several skaters emerged as likely medal hopes following the retirement of <a href="/wiki/Scott_Hamilton_(figure_skater)" title="Scott Hamilton (figure skater)">Scott Hamilton</a>.
</p><p>Boitano won the 1985 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Figure_Skating_Championships" title="United States Figure Skating Championships">United States Figure Skating Championships</a>, the first of his four titles. At the first <a href="/wiki/1985_World_Figure_Skating_Championships" title="1985 World Figure Skating Championships">World Championships</a> of the post-Hamilton era in 1985, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_Fadeev_(figure_skater)" title="Alexander Fadeev (figure skater)">Alexander Fadeev</a> won, with <a href="/wiki/Brian_Orser" title="Brian Orser">Brian Orser</a> finishing 2nd and Boitano 3rd. He had injured tendons in his right ankle a few weeks before the 1986 U.S. Championships but went on to win his second national title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-si021786_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-si021786-9">[9]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/1986_World_Figure_Skating_Championships" title="1986 World Figure Skating Championships">1986 World Championships</a>, Boitano took the title, while Fadeev had a disastrous free skate despite having been in an excellent position to win; Orser finished 2nd once again.
</p><p>During the 1986–87 season, Boitano had introduced two new elements to his programs: the 'Tano triple <a href="/wiki/Lutz_jump" title="Lutz jump">lutz</a> and a quadruple <a href="/wiki/Toe_loop_jump" title="Toe loop jump">toe loop</a>, although he never succeeded in landing a clean quadruple jump in competition. The 1987 World Championships were held in <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati" title="Cincinnati">Cincinnati</a>, giving defending World champion Boitano a home-field advantage. The outcome of the event would set the tone for the 1988 Olympics. At Worlds, Boitano fell on his quadruple toe loop attempt and placed second.
</p><p>After losing the world title to Orser at home, Boitano and his coach <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Leaver&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Linda Leaver (page does not exist)">Linda Leaver</a> decided that some changes needed to be made if Boitano was to become the Olympic champion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-passiontoskate_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-passiontoskate-10">[10]</a></sup> Boitano had always been good at the technical requirements ("The first mark"), but he was weak on the artistic ("the second mark"). He was a self-described "jumping robot." In order to help his growth as an artist, he hired choreographer <a href="/wiki/Sandra_Bezic" title="Sandra Bezic">Sandra Bezic</a> to choreograph his programs for the 1987–1988 Olympic season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-passiontoskate_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-passiontoskate-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bezic choreographed two programs that featured clean lines and accentuated the skating abilities of the 5' 11" Boitano.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-passiontoskate_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-passiontoskate-10">[10]</a></sup>  The short program was based on <a href="/wiki/Giacomo_Meyerbeer" title="Giacomo Meyerbeer">Giacomo Meyerbeer</a>'s ballet <i>Les Patineurs</i> in which Boitano plays a cocky young man showing off his tricks, using movements dating back to the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-passiontoskate_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-passiontoskate-10">[10]</a></sup> In one famous moment, Boitano wipes ice shavings, also called snow, off his skate blade and tosses it over his shoulder after landing a triple axel combination. The free skating program was based on the film score, <i>Napoleon</i>, detailing various phases of a soldier's life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-passiontoskate_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-passiontoskate-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Boitano debuted his new programs at 1987 <a href="/wiki/Skate_Canada_International" title="Skate Canada International">Skate Canada</a>, held in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saddledome" title="Saddledome">Saddledome</a> in <a href="/wiki/Calgary" title="Calgary">Calgary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, Canada, the same venue in which he would compete against Brian Orser for the Olympic title three months later. Boitano's new programs were received with standing ovations by the audience. Although Orser won the competition, Boitano skated clean, landing seven triple jumps, including a footwork section into a jump. He did however pop his planned 2nd triple axel.  Boitano, Leaver, and Bezic were so confident about the strength of Boitano's new programs that they omitted the quadruple toe loop, which if landed, could have put him a shoulder above Orser in technical merit.
</p><p>The short program at the 1988 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Figure_Skating_Championships" title="United States Figure Skating Championships">United States Figure Skating Championships</a> proved to be a highlight. Boitano received marks of 6.0 from eight of the nine judges for presentation, the second mark. His free skate was flawed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-passiontoskate_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-passiontoskate-10">[10]</a></sup> Due to delays, he did not skate until after midnight. Still, Boitano won the competition, and went into the Olympics as the national champion (U.S.), as did Orser (Canadian).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1988_Olympics:_Battle_of_the_Brians">1988 Olympics: Battle of the Brians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Boitano&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1988 Olympics: Battle of the Brians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Brians" title="Battle of the Brians">Battle of the Brians</a></div>
<p>Going into the Olympics, Boitano and <a href="/wiki/Brian_Orser" title="Brian Orser">Brian Orser</a> each had won a World title and each had an excellent, balanced repertoire, with Boitano being known as the slightly better technician and Orser as the better artist. Adding to the rivalry, Boitano and Orser were both performing military-themed programs. Boitano's was to the music of Napoleon.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Brians" title="Battle of the Brians">Battle of the Brians</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Figure_skating_at_the_1988_Winter_Olympics" title="Figure skating at the 1988 Winter Olympics">1988 Winter Olympics</a> was the highlight of Boitano's amateur career. Boitano and Orser were effectively tied going into the <a href="/wiki/Free_skating" title="Free skating">free skating</a> portion of the event and whoever won that portion would win the event. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_Vladimirovich_Fadeyev" title="Alexander Vladimirovich Fadeyev">Alexander Fadeev</a> had won the <a href="/wiki/Compulsory_figures" title="Compulsory figures">compulsory figures</a> section of the competition with Boitano second and Orser third. In the <a href="/wiki/Short_program_(figure_skating)" title="Short program (figure skating)">short program</a>, Orser placed first and Boitano second. The <a href="/wiki/Free_skating" title="Free skating">free skating</a> was, at the time, worth 50% of the score, and so Boitano's lead would not be enough to hold him in first place if he lost the free skate.
</p><p>Boitano skated a clean, technically excellent long program, with eight triple jumps, including two Axels, and a triple flip-triple toe loop combination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>  Landing his second triple Axel jump cleanly was probably a critical factor in the battle. Orser made one small mistake on a jump and omitted his planned second triple Axel. Boitano won the Battle in a 5–4 split.  With his win, he became the first Olympic champion to land the full complement of six types of triple jumps. Boitano won the gold medal, wearing skates with American flag appliqués that are now part of the collections of the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_American_History" title="National Museum of American History">National Museum of American History</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>.
</p><p>Following the Olympics, both Orser and Boitano went to the World Championships, which Boitano won. Boitano turned professional soon after.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Professional_career_and_return_to_amateur_standing">Professional career and return to amateur standing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Boitano&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Professional career and return to amateur standing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the Olympics, Boitano went on to dominate competitions in the professional ranks, winning 10 straight professional competitions, including 5 consecutive World Professional Championship titles and 4 consecutive wins at the Challenge of Champions. Boitano also appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/Carmen_on_Ice" title="Carmen on Ice">Carmen on Ice</a></i>, for which he won an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emmy" title="Emmy">Emmy</a>. However, Boitano wanted to return to amateur competition and make another run at the Olympics.
</p><p>Boitano's lobby proved successful and in June 1993, the <a href="/wiki/International_Skating_Union" title="International Skating Union">International Skating Union</a> (ISU) introduced a clause, commonly known as the "Boitano rule," which allowed professionals to reinstate as "amateur" or "eligible" skaters. This had been the result of Boitano's active involvement during the early 1990s, which saw professionals being allowed in the Olympic Games in the sports of tennis and basketball.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Boitano reinstated as an amateur to compete in the <a href="/wiki/1994_Winter_Olympics" title="1994 Winter Olympics">1994 Winter Olympics</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lillehammer" title="Lillehammer">Lillehammer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>.
</p><p>Boitano competed at the 1994 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Figure_Skating_Championships" title="United States Figure Skating Championships">United States Figure Skating Championships</a>, led after the short program, but lost to <a href="/wiki/Scott_Davis_(figure_skater)" title="Scott Davis (figure skater)">Scott Davis</a> in the long program in a 6–3 split decision. Boitano was named to the Olympic team. Going into the Olympics as a medal favorite in a strong field, Boitano missed his triple axel combination during the short program for the first time in his career. This mistake proved extremely costly, and knocked Boitano out of medal contention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> He skated a good long program and finished 6th.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Boitano returned to the professional ranks afterwards. He was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/World_Figure_Skating_Hall_of_Fame" title="World Figure Skating Hall of Fame">World Figure Skating Hall of Fame</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Figure_Skating_Hall_of_Fame" title="United States Figure Skating Hall of Fame">United States Figure Skating Hall of Fame</a> in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sr_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-sr-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Boitano&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In December 2013, Boitano was named to the United States delegation to the <a href="/wiki/2014_Winter_Olympics" title="2014 Winter Olympics">2014 Winter Olympics</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sochi" title="Sochi">Sochi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. In conjunction with that appointment, Boitano publicly <a href="/wiki/Coming_out" title="Coming out">came out</a> as gay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sieczkowski_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sieczkowski-17">[17]</a></sup> The Sochi games and Russia have been the targets of criticism and LGBT activism because of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_anti-LGBT_propaganda_law" title="Russian anti-LGBT propaganda law">Russian anti-gay "propaganda" law</a> passed in June 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloombergputinsigns_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-bloombergputinsigns-18">[18]</a></sup> In January 2014, he told <i><a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a></i> that he had never wanted to come out until the delegation announcement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Celebrity_and_popular_culture_career">Celebrity and popular culture career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Boitano&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Celebrity and popular culture career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Park_song"><i>South Park</i> song</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Boitano&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: South Park song">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/What_Would_Brian_Boitano_Do%3F" title="What Would Brian Boitano Do?">What Would Brian Boitano Do?</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Caricature" title="Caricature">caricature</a> of Boitano as a <a href="/wiki/Superhero" title="Superhero">superhero</a> appears as a semi-recurring character in the <a href="/wiki/Cartoon" title="Cartoon">cartoon</a> series <i><a href="/wiki/South_Park" title="South Park">South Park</a></i>. The movie <i><a href="/wiki/South_Park:_Bigger,_Longer_%26_Uncut" title="South Park: Bigger, Longer &amp; Uncut">South Park: Bigger, Longer &amp; Uncut</a></i> (1999), features a musical number titled "<a href="/wiki/What_Would_Brian_Boitano_Do%3F" title="What Would Brian Boitano Do?">What Would Brian Boitano Do?</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> He was also featured in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Spirit_of_Christmas_(short_film)#Jesus_vs._Santa" title="The Spirit of Christmas (short film)">Jesus vs. Santa</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_Network_show">Food Network show</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Boitano&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Food Network show">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On August 23, 2009, <a href="/wiki/Food_Network" title="Food Network">Food Network</a> debuted a new series entitled <i><a href="/wiki/What_Would_Brian_Boitano_Make%3F" title="What Would Brian Boitano Make?">What Would Brian Boitano Make?</a></i>, which borrows both its name and opening musical theme from the <i><a href="/wiki/South_Park:_Bigger,_Longer_%26_Uncut" title="South Park: Bigger, Longer &amp; Uncut">South Park: Bigger, Longer &amp; Uncut</a></i> song. The show features Boitano preparing meals for his friends. The series was picked up for a ten-episode second season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_television_and_film_appearances">Other television and film appearances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Boitano&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other television and film appearances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Boitano starred, along with <a href="/wiki/Katarina_Witt" title="Katarina Witt">Katarina Witt</a> and archrival <a href="/wiki/Brian_Orser" title="Brian Orser">Brian Orser</a>, in the 1990 German <a href="/wiki/Dance_film" title="Dance film">dance film</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Carmen_on_Ice" title="Carmen on Ice">Carmen on Ice</a></i>; Boitano played Don Jose. All three won an <a href="/wiki/Emmy_Award" title="Emmy Award">Emmy Award</a> for "Outstanding performance in classical music/dance programming".</li>
<li>He was featured in the <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_XXVI" title="Super Bowl XXVI">Super Bowl XXVI</a> halftime show "Winter Magic" along with <a href="/wiki/Gloria_Estefan" title="Gloria Estefan">Gloria Estefan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Hamill" title="Dorothy Hamill">Dorothy Hamill</a>.</li>
<li>Boitano had a <a href="/wiki/Cameo_appearance" title="Cameo appearance">cameo</a> in the 2007 film <i><a href="/wiki/Blades_of_Glory" title="Blades of Glory">Blades of Glory</a></i> as a world skating federation judge.</li>
<li>He and fellow figure skater <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Kwan" title="Michelle Kwan">Michelle Kwan</a> had a cameo as themselves in the 2005 Disney film, <i><a href="/wiki/Ice_Princess" title="Ice Princess">Ice Princess</a></i>, appearing as <a href="/wiki/Sports_commentator" title="Sports commentator">commentators</a> during the Sectionals competition.</li>
<li>He appeared on <i><a href="/wiki/Giada_at_Home" title="Giada at Home">Giada at Home</a></i> for one episode.</li>
<li>He appeared as a guest judge on Top Chef Masters Season 4 episode 3. The episode is titled "What would Brian Boitano Make?"</li>
<li>He appeared on Fox Business Network's <i>Stossel</i>, episode "Spontaneous Order" (2/10/11).</li>
<li>He appeared in an episode of <i><a href="/wiki/Check,_Please!" title="Check, Please!">Check, Please! Bay Area</a></i>, a restaurant review program which airs on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KQED-TV" title="KQED-TV">KQED-TV</a> in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>He hosted a series on HGTV, called "The Brian Boitano Project", which premiered January 16, 2014, in which he purchased a near derelict ancestral home in Northern Italy, home to many Boitanos. During the series he gives the home in <a href="/wiki/Favale_di_Malvaro" title="Favale di Malvaro">Favale di Malvaro</a> a sympathetic restoration/renovation and shops flea markets with two nieces to find decor and furnishings. Local artisans, carpenters, masons and painters create a gem where he can live part-time and host Boitanos from afar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Boitano appeared on the reality series <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hell%27s_Kitchen_(U.S._TV_series)" title="Hell's Kitchen (U.S. TV series)">Hell's Kitchen</a></i> where he along with <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Ramsay" title="Gordon Ramsay">Gordon Ramsay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Traci_Des_Jardins" title="Traci Des Jardins">Traci Des Jardins</a> co-judged the team challenge in the episode "Hell Freezes Over".</li></ul>
